<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">New religious movement</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Religious community or spiritual group of modern origin</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"New religion" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/New_religion_(disambiguation)" title="New religion (disambiguation)">New religion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg/220px-Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg/330px-Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg/440px-Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russian_Hare_Krishna_Devotee_on_Sankirtan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">International Society for Krishna Consciousness</a> <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytising</a> on the streets of <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>new religious movement</b> (<b>NRM</b>), also known as <b>alternative spirituality</b> or a <b>new religion</b>, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious" title="Religious">religious</a> or <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> group that has modern origins and is peripheral to its society's dominant religious culture. NRMs can be novel in origin or they can be part of a wider <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, in which case they are distinct from pre-existing <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">denominations</a>. Some NRMs deal with the challenges which the modernizing world poses to them by embracing <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a>, while other NRMs deal with them by embracing tightly knit collective means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarke2006a_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarke2006a-1">[1]</a></sup> Scholars have estimated that NRMs number in the tens of thousands worldwide, with most of their members living in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Most NRMs only have a few members, some of them have thousands of members, and a few of them have more than a million members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker1999_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-barker1999-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no single, agreed-upon criterion for defining a "new religious movement".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver20125–6_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver20125–6-3">[3]</a></sup> There is debate as to how the term "new" should be interpreted in this context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201214_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201214-4">[4]</a></sup> One perspective is that it should designate a religion that is more recent in its origins than large, well-established religions like <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201214_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201214-4">[4]</a></sup> An alternate perspective is that "new" should mean that a religion is more recent in its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201214_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201214-4">[4]</a></sup> Some scholars view the 1950s or the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> in 1945 as the defining time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker19896,_143_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker19896,_143-5">[5]</a></sup> while others look as far back as from the middle of the 19th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> or the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a> in 1830<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201214_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201214-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a> in 1838.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b621–623Tenrikyo_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b621–623Tenrikyo-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>New religions have typically faced opposition from established religious organisations and secular institutions. In Western nations, a secular <a href="/wiki/Anti-cult_movement" title="Anti-cult movement">anti-cult movement</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Christian_countercult_movement" title="Christian countercult movement">Christian countercult movement</a> emerged during the 1970s and 1980s to oppose emergent groups.  In the 1970s, the distinct field of new religions studies developed within the academic study of religion. There are several scholarly organisations and peer-reviewed journals devoted to the subject. Religious studies scholars contextualize the rise of NRMs in modernity as a product of, and answer to modern processes of <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detraditionalization" title="Detraditionalization">detraditionalization</a>, fragmentation, <a href="/wiki/Reflexivity_(social_theory)" title="Reflexivity (social theory)">reflexivity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarke2006a_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarke2006a-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1123" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg/220px-Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg/330px-Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg/440px-Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swami_Vivekananda_at_Parliament_of_Religions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1893 <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_World%27s_Religions" title="Parliament of the World's Religions">Parliament of the World's Religions</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1830 the <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a>. It is one of the largest new religious movements in terms of membership. In Japan, 1838 marks the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b621–623Tenrikyo_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b621–623Tenrikyo-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1844 <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1bism" title="Bábism">Bábism</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, from which the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> was founded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a> in 1863. In 1860 <a href="/wiki/Donghak" title="Donghak">Donghak</a>, later <a href="/wiki/Cheondoism" title="Cheondoism">Cheondoism</a>, was founded by <a href="/wiki/Choe_Je-u" title="Choe Je-u">Choi Jae-Woo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>. It later ignited the <a href="/wiki/Donghak_Peasant_Revolution" title="Donghak Peasant Revolution">Donghak Peasant Revolution</a> in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yao_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yao-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1889, <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">Islamic branch</a>, was founded by <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghulam_Ahmad" title="Mirza Ghulam Ahmad">Mirza Ghulam Ahmad</a>.  In 1891, the <a href="/wiki/Unity_Church" title="Unity Church">Unity Church</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/New_Thought" title="New Thought">New Thought</a> denomination, was founded in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBOnline_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBOnline-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1893, the first <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_World%27s_Religions" title="Parliament of the World's Religions">Parliament of the World's Religions</a> was held in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McRae_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-McRae-11">[11]</a></sup> The conference included NRMs of the time such as <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">spiritualism</a>, Baháʼí Faith, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Harris_Jessup" title="Henry Harris Jessup">Henry Harris Jessup</a>, who addressed the meeting, was the first to mention the Baháʼí Faith in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Also attending were <a href="/wiki/Soyen_Shaku" title="Soyen Shaku">Soyen Shaku</a>, the "First American Ancestor" of <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ford_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ford-13">[13]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therav%C4%81da" title="Theravāda">Theravāda</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> preacher <a href="/wiki/Anagarika_Dharmapala" title="Anagarika Dharmapala">Anagarika Dharmapala</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> preacher <a href="/wiki/Virchand_Gandhi" title="Virchand Gandhi">Virchand Gandhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> This conference gave Asian religious teachers their first wide American audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1911, the <a href="/wiki/Nazareth_Baptist_Church" title="Nazareth Baptist Church">Nazareth Baptist Church</a>, the first and one of the largest modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_initiated_church" title="African initiated church">African initiated churches</a>, was founded by <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Shembe" title="Isaiah Shembe">Isaiah Shembe</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The early 20th century also saw a rise in interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asatru" title="Asatru">Asatru</a>. The 1930s saw the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> in the United States; the rise of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastafari_movement" title="Rastafari movement">Rastafari movement</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>; the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cao_%C4%90%C3%A0i" title="Cao Đài">Cao Đài</a> and <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B2a_H%E1%BA%A3o" title="Hòa Hảo">Hòa Hảo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>; the rise of <a href="/wiki/Soka_Gakkai" title="Soka Gakkai">Soka Gakkai</a> in Japan; and the rise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zailiism" title="Zailiism">Zailiism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yiguandao" title="Yiguandao">Yiguandao</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. In the 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Gardner_(Wiccan)" title="Gerald Gardner (Wiccan)">Gerald Gardner</a> began to outline the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_pagan" title="Modern pagan">modern pagan</a> religion of <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>.
</p><p>New religious movements expanded in many nations in the 1950s and 1960s. Japanese new religions became very popular after the <a href="/wiki/Shinto_Directive" title="Shinto Directive">Shinto Directive</a> (1945) forced the Japanese government to separate itself from <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, which had been the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of Japan, bringing about greater <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>. In 1954 <a href="/wiki/Scientology" title="Scientology">Scientology</a> was founded in the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Unification_Church" title="Unification Church">Unification Church</a> was founded in <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1955 the <a href="/wiki/Aetherius_Society" title="Aetherius Society">Aetherius Society</a> was founded in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. It and some other NRMs, have been called <a href="/wiki/UFO_religion" title="UFO religion">UFO religions</a> because they combine the belief in <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> with traditional religious principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_R._Lewis_(ed.)1995_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_R._Lewis_(ed.)1995-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1965, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Twitchell" title="Paul Twitchell">Paul Twitchell</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Eckankar" title="Eckankar">Eckankar</a>, an NRM derived partially from <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Sant_Mat_movements" title="Contemporary Sant Mat movements">Sant Mat</a>. In 1966 the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">International Society for Krishna Consciousness</a> was founded in the United States by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.C._Bhaktivedanta_Swami_Prabhupada" title="A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada">A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Anton_LaVey" title="Anton LaVey">Anton LaVey</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Satan" title="Church of Satan">Church of Satan</a>. In 1967, <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>' <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_in_India" title="The Beatles in India">visit</a> to <a href="/wiki/Maharishi_Mahesh_Yogi" title="Maharishi Mahesh Yogi">Maharishi Mahesh Yogi</a> in India brought public attention to the <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation_movement" title="Transcendental Meditation movement">Transcendental Meditation movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_den_Berg_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_den_Berg-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corder_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corder-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1611" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg/220px-Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg/330px-Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg/440px-Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Origins-GuangzhouPractice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Practitioners of Falun Gong perform spiritual exercises in <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, China.</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 1980s and 1990s, the decline of <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">revolutions of 1989</a> opened up new opportunities for NRMs. <a href="/wiki/Falun_Gong" title="Falun Gong">Falun Gong</a> was first taught publicly in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China" title="Northeast China">Northeast China</a> in 1992 by <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhi" title="Li Hongzhi">Li Hongzhi</a>. At first it was accepted by the Chinese government and by 1999 there were 70 million practitioners in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> but in July 1999 the government started to view the movement as a threat and <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Falun_Gong" title="Persecution of Falun Gong">began attempts to eradicate it</a>.
</p><p>In the 21st century, many NRMs are using the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> to give out information, to recruit members, and sometimes to hold online meetings and rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup> That is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Cybersectarianism" title="Cybersectarianism">cybersectarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sabina_Magliocco" title="Sabina Magliocco">Sabina Magliocco</a>, professor of Anthropology and Folklore at <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Northridge" title="California State University, Northridge">California State University, Northridge</a>, has discussed the growing popularity of new religious movements on the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006 <a href="/wiki/J._Gordon_Melton" title="J. Gordon Melton">J. Gordon Melton</a>, executive director of the Institute for the Study of American Religions at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara" title="University of California, Santa Barbara">University of California, Santa Barbara</a>, told <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> that 40 to 45 new religious movements emerge each year in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 2007, religious scholar <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Siegler" title="Elijah Siegler">Elijah Siegler</a> said that, though no NRM had become the dominant faith in any country, many of the concepts they first introduced (often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a>" ideas) have become part of worldwide mainstream culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_practices">Beliefs and practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Beliefs and practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg/220px-Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg/330px-Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg/440px-Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rasta_Man_Barbados.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Rasta man wearing symbols of his religious identity in <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a></div></div></div>
<p>As noted by Barker, NRMs should not be "lumped together". They differ from one another on many issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker19891_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker19891-28">[28]</a></sup>
Virtually no generalisation can be made about NRMs that applies to every group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198910_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198910-29">[29]</a></sup> with Barrett noting that "generalizations tend not to be very helpful" when studying NRMs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett20019_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett20019-30">[30]</a></sup> Melton expressed the view that there is "no single characteristic or set of characteristics" that all new religions share, "not even their newness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton200476_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton200476-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bryan_R._Wilson" title="Bryan R. Wilson">Bryan Wilson</a> wrote, "Chief among the miss-directed assertions has been the tendency to speak of new religious movements as if they differed very little if at all, one from another. The tendency has been to lump them altogether and indiscriminately to attribute to all of the characteristics which are, in fact, valid for only one or two."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> NRMs themselves often claim that they exist at a crucial place in time and space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20126_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20126-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scriptures">Scriptures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scriptures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some NRMs venerate unique <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">scriptures</a>, while others reinterpret existing texts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> utilizing a range of older elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20127_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20127-35">[35]</a></sup> They frequently claim that these are not new, but rather had been forgotten truths that are being revived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20128_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20128-36">[36]</a></sup>  NRM scriptures often incorporate modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific">scientific</a> knowledge, sometimes with the claim that they are bringing unity to science and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>  Some NRMs believe that their scriptures are received through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediums" title="Mediums">mediums</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Urantia_Book" title="The Urantia Book">The Urantia Book</a></i>, the core scripture of the Urantia Movement, was published in 1955 and is said to be the product of a continuous process of <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> from "celestial beings" which began in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardner199511_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardner199511-39">[39]</a></sup>  Some NRMs, particularly those that are forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">occultism</a>, have a prescribed system of courses and grades through which members can progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200157_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200157-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celibacy">Celibacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Celibacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some NRMs promote <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibacy</a>, the state of voluntarily being unmarried, sexually abstinent, or both.  Some, including the <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a> and more recent NRMs, inspired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> traditions, see it as a lifelong commitment. Others, including the <a href="/wiki/Unification_Church" title="Unification Church">Unification Church</a>, as a stage in spiritual development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>  In some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> NRMs, celibacy is practiced mostly by older women who become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuns" title="Nuns">nuns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Some people join NRMs and practice celibacy as a <a href="/wiki/Rite_of_passage" title="Rite of passage">rite of passage</a> in order to move beyond previous sexual problems or bad experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>  Groups that promote celibacy require a strong recruitment drive to survive; the Shakers established orphanages to bring new individuals into their community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200167_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200167-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence">Violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Violent incidents involving NRMs are very rare. In events having a large number of casualties, the new religion was led by a charismatic leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200183_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200183-45">[45]</a></sup>  Beginning in 1978 the deaths of 913 members of the <a href="/wiki/Peoples_Temple" title="Peoples Temple">Peoples Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jonestown" title="Jonestown">Jonestown</a>, Guyana by both murder and suicide brought an image of "killer cults" to public attention. Several subsequent events contributed to the concept.  In 1994, members of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Solar_Temple" title="Order of the Solar Temple">Order of the Solar Temple</a> committed suicide in Canada and Switzerland. In 1995 members of the Japanese new religion <a href="/wiki/Aum_Shinrikyo" title="Aum Shinrikyo">Aum Shinrikyo</a> murdered a number of people during a <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_subway_sarin_attack" title="Tokyo subway sarin attack">sarin attack on the Tokyo subway</a>. In 1997, 39 members of the <a href="/wiki/Heaven%27s_Gate_(religious_group)" title="Heaven's Gate (religious group)">Heaven's Gate</a> group committed suicide in the belief that their spirits would leave the Earth and join a passing comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200182_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200182-46">[46]</a></sup>   There have also been cases in which members of NRMs have been killed after they engaged in dangerous actions due to mistaken belief in their own invincibility.  For example, in Uganda, several hundred members of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_Movement" title="Holy Spirit Movement">Holy Spirit Movement</a> were killed as they approached gunfire because its leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alice_Lakwena" title="Alice Lakwena">Alice Lakwena</a>, told them that they would be protected from bullets by the oil of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shea_tree" title="Shea tree">shea tree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198955_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198955-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_and_succession">Leadership and succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Leadership and succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1062" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg/220px-Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg/330px-Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg/440px-Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Baker_Eddy_cph.3b20582.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mary_Baker_Eddy" title="Mary Baker Eddy">Mary Baker Eddy</a></div></div></div>
<p>NRMs are typically founded and led by a charismatic leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198913_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198913-48">[48]</a></sup> The death of any religion's founder represents a significant moment in its history.   Over the months and years following its leader's death, the movement can die out, fragment into multiple groups, consolidate its position, or change its nature to become something quite different than what its founder intended. In some cases, an NRM moves closer to the religious mainstream after the death of its founder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200158–60_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200158–60-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of founders of new religions established plans for succession to prevent confusion after their deaths.   <a href="/wiki/Mary_Baker_Eddy" title="Mary Baker Eddy">Mary Baker Eddy</a>, the American founder of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a>, spent fifteen years working on her book <i>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manual_of_the_Mother_Church" title="Manual of the Mother Church">Manual of the Mother Church</a></i>, which laid out how the group should be run by her successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200166_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200166-50">[50]</a></sup> 
The leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> passed through a succession of individuals until 1963, when it was assumed by the <a href="/wiki/Universal_House_of_Justice" title="Universal House of Justice">Universal House of Justice</a>, members of which are elected by the worldwide congregation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.C._Bhaktivedanta_Swami_Prabhupada" title="A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada">A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">International Society for Krishna Consciousness</a>, appointed 11 "Western Gurus" to act as initiating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurus" title="Gurus">gurus</a> and to continue to direct the organisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0310232171_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0310232171-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-941532-57-7_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-941532-57-7-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-8135-1114-3_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-8135-1114-3-55">[55]</a></sup> However, according to British scholar of religion <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Flood" title="Gavin Flood">Gavin Flood</a>, "many problems followed from their appointment and the movement has since veered away from investing absolute authority in a few, fallible, human teachers.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood1996_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood1996-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>NRMs typically consist largely of first-generation believers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198911_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198911-57">[57]</a></sup> and thus often have a younger average membership than mainstream religious congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198911–12_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198911–12-58">[58]</a></sup>  Some NRMs have been formed by groups who have split from a pre-existing religious group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198913_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198913-48">[48]</a></sup> As these members grow older, many have children who are then brought up within the NRM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198912_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198912-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>, NRMs most often appeal to the poor and oppressed sectors of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198914_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198914-60">[60]</a></sup>  Within Western countries, they are more likely to appeal to members of the middle and upper-middle classes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198914_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198914-60">[60]</a></sup> with Barrett stating that new religions in the UK and US largely attract "white, middle-class late teens and twenties."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200198_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200198-61">[61]</a></sup>  There are exceptions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rastafari" title="Rastafari">Rastafari</a> movement and the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>,  which have primarily attracted disadvantaged black youth in Western countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198914_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198914-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>A popular conception, unsupported by evidence, holds that those who convert to new religions are either mentally ill or become so through their involvement with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198955–56_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198955–56-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dick_Anthony" title="Dick Anthony">Dick Anthony</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensic_psychologist" title="Forensic psychologist">forensic psychologist</a> noted for his writings on the <a href="/wiki/Brainwashing" title="Brainwashing">brainwashing</a> controversy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> has defended NRMs, and in 1988 argued that involvement in such movements may often be beneficial: "There's a large research literature published in mainstream journals on the mental health effects of new religions. For the most part, the effects seem to be positive in any way that's measurable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sipchen_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sipchen-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Joining">Joining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Joining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Those who convert to an NRM typically believe that in doing so they are gaining some benefit in their life. This can come in many forms, from an increasing sense of freedom to a release from drug dependency, and a feeling of self-respect and direction. Many of those who have left NRMs report that they have gained from their experience. There are various reasons as to why an individual would join and then remain part of an NRM, including both push and pull factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198925–26_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198925–26-66">[66]</a></sup>  According to <a href="/wiki/Marc_Galanter_(psychiatrist)" title="Marc Galanter (psychiatrist)">Marc Galanter</a>, professor of psychiatry at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NYU" title="NYU">NYU</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> typical reasons why people join NRMs include a search for community and a spiritual quest. Sociologists <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Stark" title="Rodney Stark">Stark</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Sims_Bainbridge" title="William Sims Bainbridge">Bainbridge</a>, in discussing the process by which people join new religious groups, have questioned the utility of the concept of <i>conversion</i>, suggesting that <i>affiliation</i> is a more useful concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>A popular explanation for why people join new religious movements is that they have been "<a href="/wiki/Brainwashing" title="Brainwashing">brainwashed</a>" or subject to "mind control" by the NRM itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198917_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198917-69">[69]</a></sup> This explanation provides a rationale for "deprogramming", a process in which members of NRMs are illegally kidnapped by individuals who then attempt to convince them to reject their beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198917_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198917-69">[69]</a></sup> Professional deprogrammers, therefore, have a financial interest in promoting the "brainwashing" explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198919_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198919-70">[70]</a></sup> Academic research, however, has demonstrated that these brainwashing techniques "simply do not exist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20123_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20123-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaving">Leaving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Leaving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many members of NRMs leave these groups of their own free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198918–19_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198918–19-72">[72]</a></sup> Some of those who do so retain friends within the movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200147_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200147-73">[73]</a></sup>  Some of those who leave a religious community are unhappy with the time that they spent as part of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200147_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200147-73">[73]</a></sup>  Leaving a NRM can pose a number of difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200154_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200154-74">[74]</a></sup> It may result in their having to abandon a daily framework that they had previously adhered to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200155_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200155-75">[75]</a></sup> It may also generate mixed emotions as ex-members lose the feelings of absolute certainty that they had held while in the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200154_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200154-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_scholarship">Academic scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Academic scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Academic_study_of_new_religious_movements" title="Academic study of new religious movements">Academic study of new religious movements</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Three basic questions have been paramount in orienting theory and research on NRMs: what are the identifying markers of NRMs that distinguish them from other types of religious groups?; what are the different types of NRMs and how do these different types relate to the established institutional order of the host society?; and what are the most important ways that NRMs respond to the sociocultural dislocation that leads to their formation?
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Sociologist of religion <a href="/wiki/David_G._Bromley" title="David G. Bromley">David G. Bromley</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromley201214_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromley201214-76">[76]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The academic study of new religious movements is known as 'new religions studies' (NRS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromley200483Bromley201213_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromley200483Bromley201213-77">[77]</a></sup>
The study draws from the disciplines of <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teaching_bromley_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Teaching_bromley-78">[78]</a></sup>  Barker noted that there are five sources of information on NRMs: the information provided by such groups themselves, that provided by ex-members as well as the friends and relatives of members, organisations that collect information on NRMs, the mainstream media, and academics studying such phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker1989vii–ix_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker1989vii–ix-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of new religions is unified by its topic of interest, rather than by its <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodology</a>, and is therefore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interdisciplinary" title="Interdisciplinary">interdisciplinary</a> in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis20048Melton2004b16_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis20048Melton2004b16-80">[80]</a></sup> A sizeable body of scholarly literature on new religions has been published, most of it produced by <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social scientists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromley201213HammerRothstein20122_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromley201213HammerRothstein20122-81">[81]</a></sup> Among the disciplines that NRS utilises are anthropology, history, psychology, religious studies, and sociology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromley201213_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromley201213-82">[82]</a></sup> Of these approaches, sociology played a particularly prominent role in the development of the field,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromley201213_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromley201213-82">[82]</a></sup> resulting in it being initially confined largely to a narrow array of sociological questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20125_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20125-83">[83]</a></sup> This came to change in later scholarship, which began to apply theories and methods initially developed for examining more mainstream religions to the study of new ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20125_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20125-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most research has been directed toward those new religions that attract public controversy. Less controversial NRMs tend to be the subject of less scholarly research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b20_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b20-84">[84]</a></sup> It has also been noted that scholars of new religions often avoid researching certain movements that scholars from other backgrounds study. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feminist_spirituality" title="Feminist spirituality">feminist spirituality</a> movement is usually examined by scholars of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_studies" title="Women's studies">women's studies</a>, African-American new religions by scholars of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africana_studies" title="Africana studies">Africana studies</a>, and Native American new religions by scholars of <a href="/wiki/Native_American_studies" title="Native American studies">Native American studies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis20048_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis20048-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_terminology">Definitions and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Definitions and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1512" data-file-width="2016" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/220px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/330px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG/440px-Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rainbow_Gathering_Bosnia_2007.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Gathering" title="Rainbow Gathering">Rainbow Gathering</a> in Bosnia, 2007</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/J._Gordon_Melton" title="J. Gordon Melton">J. Gordon Melton</a> argued that "new religious movements" should be defined by the way dominant religious and secular forces within a given society treat them. According to him, NRMs constituted "those religious groups that have been found, from the perspective of the dominant religious community (and in the West that is almost always a form of Christianity), to be not just different, but unacceptably different."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton200479_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton200479-86">[86]</a></sup> Barker cautioned against Melton's approach, arguing that negating the "newness" of "new religious movements" raises problems, for it is "the very fact that NRMs are new that explains many of the key characteristics they display".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker200489_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker200489-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Chryssides" title="George Chryssides">George Chryssides</a> favors "simple" definition, for him, NRM is an organization founded within the past 150 or so years, which cannot be easily classified within one of the world's main religious traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDriedgerWolfart20185–12_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDriedgerWolfart20185–12-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars of religion <a href="/wiki/Olav_Hammer" title="Olav Hammer">Olav Hammer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikael_Rothstein" title="Mikael Rothstein">Mikael Rothstein</a> argued that "new religions are just young religions" and as a result, they are "not inherently different" from mainstream and established religious movements, with the differences between the two having been greatly exaggerated by the media and popular perceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20123_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20123-71">[71]</a></sup> Melton has stated that those NRMs that "were offshoots of older religious groups... tended to resemble their parent groups far more than they resembled each other."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton200476_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton200476-31">[31]</a></sup>  One question that faces scholars of religion is when a new religious movement ceases to be "new."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker200499_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker200499-89">[89]</a></sup>   As noted by Barker, "In the first century, Christianity was new, in the seventh century Islam was new, in the eighteenth century Methodism was new, in the nineteenth century the Seventh-day Adventists, Christadelphians, and Jehovah's Witnesses were new; in the twenty-first century the Unification Church, the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">ISKCON</a>, and Scientology are beginning to look old."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker200499_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker200499-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some NRMs are strongly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-culture" title="Counter-culture">counter-cultural</a> and 'alternative' in the society where they appear, while others are far more similar to a society's established traditional religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver20125_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver20125-90">[90]</a></sup> Generally, Christian denominations are not seen as new religious movements; nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a> have been studied as NRMs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERubinstein_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERubinstein-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-92">[92]</a></sup> The same situation with <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish religious movements</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a> and newer divisions have been named among NRM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b525–526Reform_Judaism_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarke2006b525–526Reform_Judaism-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also problems in the use of "religion" within the term "new religious movements".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201214–15_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201214–15-94">[94]</a></sup> This is because various groups, particularly active within the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> milieu, have many traits in common with different NRMs but emphasise <a href="/wiki/Personal_development" title="Personal development">personal development</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">humanistic psychology</a>, and are not clearly  "religious" in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201215_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201215-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since at least the early 2000s, most sociologists of religion have used the term "new religious movement" in order to avoid the pejorative undertones of terms like "<a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">cult</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Sect" title="Sect">sect</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200124_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200124-96">[96]</a></sup> These are words that have been used in different ways by different groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200119_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200119-97">[97]</a></sup> For instance, from the nineteenth century onward a number of sociologists used the terms "cult" and "sect" in very specific ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200123_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200123-98">[98]</a></sup> The sociologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Troeltsch" title="Ernst Troeltsch">Ernst Troeltsch</a> for instance differentiated "churches" from "sect" by claiming that the former term should apply to groups that stretch across social strata while "sects" typically contain converts from socially disadvantaged sectors of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200123_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200123-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "cult" is used in reference to devotion or dedication to a particular person or place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200121_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200121-99">[99]</a></sup> For instance, within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> devotion to <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Mary, mother of Jesus</a> is usually termed the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_of_Mary" title="Cult of Mary">Cult of Mary</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200121–22_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200121–22-100">[100]</a></sup> It is also used in non-religious contexts to refer to <a href="/wiki/Fandom" title="Fandom">fandoms</a> devoted to television shows like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prisoner" title="The Prisoner">The Prisoner</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_X-Files" title="The X-Files">The X-Files</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Buffy_the_Vampire_Slayer" title="Buffy the Vampire Slayer">Buffy the Vampire Slayer</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200122_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200122-101">[101]</a></sup> In the United States, people began to use "cult" in a pejorative manner, to refer to <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a> during the 1890s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b17_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b17-102">[102]</a></sup> As commonly used, for instance in sensationalist tabloid articles, the term "cult" continues to have pejorative associations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200120_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200120-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "new religions" is a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinshūkyō</i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">新宗教</span></span>)</span>, a Japanese term developed to describe the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">Japanese new religions</a> in the years following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis20043Melton2004b19_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis20043Melton2004b19-104">[104]</a></sup> From Japan this term was translated and used by several American authors, including <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Needleman" title="Jacob Needleman">Jacob Needleman</a>, to describe the range of groups that appeared in the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area" title="San Francisco Bay Area">San Francisco Bay Area</a> during the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b19_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2004b19-105">[105]</a></sup> This term, amongst others, was adopted by Western scholars as an alternative to "cult".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallagher,_Eugene_V_2007_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher,_Eugene_V_2007-106">[106]</a></sup> However, "new religious movements" has failed to gain widespread public usage in the manner that "cult" has.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver20126_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver20126-107">[107]</a></sup> Other terms that have been employed for many NRMs are "alternative religion" and "alternative spirituality", something used to convey the difference between these groups and established or mainstream religious movements while at the same time evading the problem posed by groups that are not particularly new.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200124Oliver201213_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200124Oliver201213-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1970s was the era of the so-called "cult wars," led by "cult-watching groups."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The efforts of the anti-cult movement condensed a <a href="/wiki/Moral_panic" title="Moral panic">moral panic</a> around the concept of cults. Public fears around <a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanism</a>, in particular, came to be known as a distinct phenomenon, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satanic_ritual_abuse" title="Satanic ritual abuse">Satanic Panic</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Consequently, scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Eileen_Barker" title="Eileen Barker">Eileen Barker</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_T._Richardson" title="James T. Richardson">James T. Richardson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Miller" title="Timothy Miller">Timothy Miller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Wessinger" title="Catherine Wessinger">Catherine Wessinger</a> argued that the term "cult" had become too laden with negative connotations, and "advocated dropping its use in academia." A number of alternatives to the term "new religious movement" are used by some scholars. These include "alternative religious movements" (Miller), "emergent religions" (Ellwood) and "marginal religious movements" (Harper and Le Beau).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cult" title="Cult">Cult</a></div>
<p>There has been opposition to NRMs throughout their history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallagher2004_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-gallagher2004-111">[111]</a></sup> Some historical events have been: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Mormonism" title="Anti-Mormonism">Anti-Mormonism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Persecution of Jehovah's Witnesses">persecution of Jehovah's Witnesses</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%ADs" title="Persecution of Baháʼís">persecution of Baháʼís</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-affolter_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-affolter-114">[114]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Falun_Gong" title="Persecution of Falun Gong">persecution of Falun Gong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orgharv_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-orgharv-115">[115]</a></sup> There are also instances in which violence has been directed at new religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198943_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198943-116">[116]</a></sup> In the United States the founder of the Latter Day Saint movement, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a>, was killed by a <a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynch mob</a> in 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DQDM_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-DQDM-117">[117]</a></sup> In India there have been mob killings of members of the <a href="/wiki/Ananda_Marga" title="Ananda Marga">Ananda Marga</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198943_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198943-116">[116]</a></sup> Such violence can also be administered by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198943_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198943-116">[116]</a></sup>  In Iran, followers of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> have faced persecution, while the <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> have faced similar violence in Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198943–44_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198943–44-118">[118]</a></sup>   Since 1999, the persecution of Falun Gong in China has been severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orgharv_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-orgharv-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amnesty1_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty1-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ethan_Gutmann" title="Ethan Gutmann">Ethan Gutmann</a> interviewed over 100 witnesses and estimated that 65,000 Falun Gong practitioners were killed for their organs from 2000 to 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jay_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jay-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viv_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Viv-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slaughter_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slaughter-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tstar_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tstar-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_countercult_movement">Christian countercult movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Christian countercult movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_countercult_movement" title="Christian countercult movement">Christian countercult movement</a></div>
<p>In the 1930s, Christian critics of NRMs began referring to them as "cults".  The 1938 book <i>The Chaos of Cults</i> by Jan Karel van Baalen (1890–1968), an ordained minister in the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Reformed_Church_in_North_America" title="Christian Reformed Church in North America">Christian Reformed Church in North America</a>, was especially influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Research_Institute" title="Christian Research Institute">Christian Research Institute</a> was founded in 1960 by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Ralston_Martin" title="Walter Ralston Martin">Walter Martin</a> to counter opposition to evangelical Christianity and has come to focus on criticisms of NRMs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett2001104–105_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett2001104–105-125">[125]</a></sup> Presently the Christian countercult movement opposes most NRMs because of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theological</a> differences.  It is closely associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christianity" title="Evangelical Christianity">evangelical Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200197_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200197-126">[126]</a></sup> The UK-based <a href="/wiki/Reachout_Trust" title="Reachout Trust">Reachout Trust</a> was initially established to oppose the <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> and what it regarded as "counterfeit Christian groups",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Inline_citation#When_you_must_use_inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Inline citation"><span title="The text near this tag needs a citation. (May 2018)">quote citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but it came to wider attention in the late 1980s and 1990s for its role in promoting claims about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satanic_ritual_abuse" title="Satanic ritual abuse">Satanic ritual abuse</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-cult_movement">Anti-cult movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Anti-cult movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-cult_movement" title="Anti-cult movement">Anti-cult movement</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The 1960s and 1970s saw the emergence of a number of highly visible new religious movements... [These] seemed so outlandish that many people saw them as evil cults, fraudulent organizations or scams that recruited unaware people by means of mind-control techniques. Real or serious religions, it was felt, should appear in recognizable institutionalized forms, be suitably ancient, and – above all – advocate relatively familiar theological notions and modes of conduct. Most new religions failed to comply with such standards.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Religious studies scholars Olav Hammer and Mikael Rothstein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20122_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammerRothstein20122-127">[127]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>In the 1970s and 1980s some NRMs,  as well as some non-religious groups, came under opposition by the newly organized anti-cult movement, which mainly charged them with <a href="/wiki/Psychological_abuse" title="Psychological abuse">psychological abuse</a> of their own members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegler2007_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-siegler2007-7">[7]</a></sup> It actively seeks to discourage people from joining new religions (which it refers to as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cults" title="Cults">cults</a>"). It also encourages members of these groups to leave them, and at times seeking to restrict their freedom of movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200197_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200197-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Family members are often distressed when a relative of theirs joins a new religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200141_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200141-128">[128]</a></sup>  Although children break away from their parents for all manner of reasons, in cases where NRMS are involved it is often the latter that are blamed for the break.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200145–46_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200145–46-129">[129]</a></sup> Some anti-cultist groups emphasise the idea that "cults" always use deceit and trickery to recruit members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200129_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200129-130">[130]</a></sup> The anti-cult movement adopted the term <i>brainwashing</i>, which had been developed by the journalist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_Hunter_(U.S._journalist)" title="Edward Hunter (U.S. journalist)">Edward Hunter</a> and then used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_J._Lifton" title="Robert J. Lifton">Robert J. Lifton</a> to apply to the methods employed by Chinese to convert captured US soldiers to their cause in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>. Lifton himself had doubts about the applicability of his 'brainwashing' hypothesis to the techniques used by NRMs to convert recruits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett200130_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett200130-131">[131]</a></sup> A number of ex-members of various new religions have made false allegations about their experiences in such groups. For instance, in the late 1980s a man in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, Ireland was given a three-year <a href="/wiki/Suspended_sentence" title="Suspended sentence">suspended sentence</a> for falsely claiming that he had been drugged, kidnapped, and held captive by members of ISKCON.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarker198939_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarker198939-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars of religion have often critiqued anti-cult groups of un-critically believing anecdotal stories provided by the ex-members of new religions, of encouraging ex-members to think that they are the victims of manipulation and abuse, and of irresponsibly scare-mongering about NRMs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett2001108_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett2001108-133">[133]</a></sup> Of the "well over a thousand groups that have been or might be called cults" listed in the files of <a href="/wiki/INFORM" title="INFORM">INFORM</a>, says Eileen Barker, the "vast majority" have not engaged in criminal activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture_and_news_media">Popular culture and news media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Popular culture and news media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movements_and_cults_in_popular_culture" title="New religious movements and cults in popular culture">New religious movements and cults in popular culture</a></div>
<p>New religious movements and cults have appeared as themes or subjects in <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, while notable representatives of such groups have produced a large body of literary works.  Beginning in the 1700s authors in the English-speaking world began introducing members of "cults" as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antagonists" title="Antagonists">antagonists</a>. In the twentieth century, concern for the rights and feelings of religious minorities led authors to most often invent fictional cults for their villains to be members of.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Fictional cults continue to be popular in film, television, and gaming in the same way, while some popular works treat new religious movements in a serious manner.
</p><p>An article on the categorization of new religious movements in US print media published by <i>The Association for the Sociology of Religion</i> (formerly the American <i>Catholic Sociological Society</i>), criticizes the print media for failing to recognize social-scientific efforts in the area of new religious movements, and its tendency to use popular or anti-cultist definitions rather than social-scientific insight, and asserts that "The failure of the print media to recognize social-scientific efforts in the area of religious movement organizations impels us to add yet another failing mark to the media report card Weiss (1985) has constructed to assess the media's reporting of the social sciences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_religious_movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
